Use unsaturated fat for dressings and dips. Unsaturated fats can be easily damaged and oxidized when heated, but are supremely healthy in their organic, extra virgin, cold-pressed form: Avocado oil Macadamia nut oil Olive oil Sesame oil Walnut oil
Use saturated fat for high heat cooking. These fats can tolerate higher heat temperatures without becoming oxidized. Buy organic, unrefined forms, from grass-fed sources if animal based, whenever possible: Coconut oil Palm oil Animal fat (from beef, lamb, chicken, or pork) Butter Ghee

I learned years ago that spices will deteriorate in heat.  Parsley, dill and other green spices will turn brown and then are totally useless.  I got tired of having to run to the store every time I needed a spice ingredient so I started storing my spices on the door in my refrigerator freezer.    The advantages are (1) you can see what you have, (2) nothing ever goes bad, and (3) spices kept in the freezer are twice as potent so you only need HALF of what the recipe calls for.  Yes, it works every time.  And when spring rolls around I buy fresh dill, parsley, scallions, rosemary, etc. dry them, roll them between wax paper with a rolling pin, put them in glass or plastic jars in the freezer and save a ton of money on really inexpensive spices that are overpriced in the grocery store.  And you never have to run out.  What could be more convenient and frugal?

The problem with using the Cornucopia Institute as a benchmark for if Kirkland/Costco eggs are good or bad is that their rating system is horribly flawed. The Institute rates them based on responses to surveys that were sent to the producers. Since Costco didn't respond, the Institute gave them all zeros. The Institute list was originally produced almost 10 years ago. They provide zero details on when they surveyed producers or when they resurveyed them, if ever. And assigning a zero to a non-respondent in a flawed methodology. I've seen greater scientific rigor in a 6th-grade science fair project. Love your channel and Instagram feed, but please investigate the "sources" you are using before citing them. And please stop telling people to not buy Kirkland/Costo eggs based on flawed and misleading information. Also, Kirkland eggs now carry the Certified Humane certification as well.

This was great with lots of good tips. I have one serious beef with what you said which was about the salt in Kerry Gold butter. People obsess over the salt in butter, OMG. The salt in that butter is a natural preservative to prevent the butter from going rancid and it will go rancid easily and quickly, even in the refrigerator without salt. You have to freeze it to prevent that. And the amount of salt that is required is a tiny, tiny amount and shouldn't affect your cooking especially if you go by taste rather than following a recipe and adding a specific quantity of salt. I almost never use the amount of salt specified in a recipe. Usually, you need more or less depending on many other factors, like whether you are using salted butter.

There is nothing unhealthy about eating Costco Rotisserie Chicken in moderation. The nutrition facts for the Costco rotisserie chicken: 3 ounces is 140 calories, 7 g fat, 2.5 g saturated fat, 0 g carbohydrates, 5.6 g sugar, 19 g protein, 0 g fiber, 460 mg sodium.. It may be a tad high in sugar but not in any way an unhealthy level, The sodium is probably less than what you would add at home with a shaker.   It is a time saver and the reason why it is cheap is because they sell it at a loss to get people in the store. They reported they lost millions just last year on them. They are now buying their own supply chain all the way from farm to slaughterhouse and the distribution networks ...the whole shabang! That will allow them to keep the price low. All Mono and Polyunsaturated oils no matter who makes it or what kind it is changes to Saturated fat once you put it in a pan on the stove or in the oven and heat it up. All Mono and Polyunsaturated oils are meant to be use in the raw state such as in salad dressings or used to drizzle on grilled steak, fish or chicken after it is grilled.  If you are going to use it to cook foods you should use a pump bottle and just squirt a small amount on each side of the food before cooking. Heat will change the molecular structure of any good oil fats to Saturated fats. Even toasting seeds and nuts over heat changes their good fat to saturated fat.

Tell the truth about American butter. Donâ€™t lie. The difference between American butter & European butter is: American butter is whipped from heavy cream, the liquid left over after the butter is removed is buttermilk. Clean & simple. European butter starts with buttermilk & sour cream. The buttermilk & sour cream are mixed together & put in the fridge to ferment overnight. Then the next day itâ€™s whipped until the butter comes out of the solution.  So, the difference is American is made from pure heavy cream; Euro-butter is made from a fermented buttermilk/sour cream mixture. European butter uses what we consider a waste product from American butter! Tell the truth. Another thing, why arenâ€™t you feeling sorry for the poor pigs that gave their life for the thick bacon? Itâ€™s all about what you like isnâ€™t it? TELL THE TRUTH!

The USDA addresses  issues of animal living conditions. USDA regulations mandate that livestock, including chicken, labeled "organic" must be allowed "access to pasture" during raising. This requirement includes "access to the outside, direct sunlight, fresh air and freedom of movement."  So Costco's Organic eggs are good because all organic eggs are required to meet this free range requirement.

Roasting your own chicken thighs sounds good. However, how can you be sure that the chicken thighs aren't from CHICKENS that are pumped full of hormones, antibiotics, etc? If you are buying the organic chicken thighs, you pay like 50% or more.  Also, the "toxic" chemicals are pretty far down on the ingredient list. If you don't know how ingredient list works, I suggest you to research on it. Of course, who wouldn't want cage free organic free range chicken? But given the "toxic" chemicals are at the end of the ingredient list, I wouldn't pay too much attention because they are quite insignificant. On top of it, it isn't like you are inhaling 10 Costco chickens a day. Moderation is the key. If they were the top 5 items on the list, it would be a different story. If you want to pay more and spend time to cook your chicken, more power to you. But paying $4.99 for a whole large cooked delicious chicken is a pretty good deal. If I may say, you wouldn't find that anywhere else in town. More than often, you pay more for a smaller less tasty cooked chicken. Bobby seems like a nice guy, and well-intentioned as all get out. But if you do all your shopping at Costco, Whole foods and/or Trader Joe's, then you either are spending too much of your hard-earned money on yuppy, (aka hipster) chow, or you're trying too hard to eat healthy.   Look, it's not that hard. In a grocery store, any grocery store, shop the periphery. Stay out of the aisles. Eat whole foods, (aka real foods). 80% of food in all food stores is processed, and processed foods contain preservatives and added sugar. The amount of sugar, regardless of form, that you need to survive is equal to goose-egg. Zero. Zip, None. Nada. The same is true for carbohydrates that aren't sugar, (starches). If all you ate was either protein of fat you'd get by just fine. If that statement wasn't true, there'd be no Inuits, (aka Eskimos). They've lived eons without carbs, (sugar or starch), and did just fine.   You want "clean food"? Grow your own or buy directly from someone who grows it. Farm to table. No middleman. The only reason to have "prepared foods" in your house is as insurance against hard times. You own that stuff with long shelf life for the days, (should they come), that going to the farm where you shop isn't an option. Think of what that looks like. Flood, hurricane, earthquake, civil unrest, whatever. They won't last forever, but having the option to NOT leave your house at such times may be the smartest prep you can do.   Be ready, be wise, be healthy, be safe. Walk in Peace. Right wrongs no one. Live long and prosper.

Your explanation about the difference between the European butter and the American butter is wrong because the difference isn't the fat content but the fact that European butter comes from pasture grazing cattle. You can actually tell the difference by the color and flavor. The American butter is pale and mild in taste due to excessive corn and hay feeding, in comparison to the European butter which is made from chlorophyll rich cream. The rotisserie chicken is not priced low because it's unnutritious but because megastores like Costco, purchase in larger quantities thereby passing the savings down to customers. Pricing the chicken low also sells it faster and curbs food waste. Lastly, the sugar and all the ingredients listed on the label are the poultry-brining ingredients.

Tip on the spices- next time you & a friend or two are at your market, pick up a brand you like (yes, even if it's a little more expensive) that comes in a glass container (you're paying for that little jar). Then, when you do go to Costco, split the cost of that big container, refill the little glass jars from it and toss it into the recycling. Why glass? Easier to clean, they clean more thoroughly and will last longer. Also with glass, it's easier to remove the previous label, if you so desire.

I buy a lot of food at Costco because of the good prices but one thing I don't buy is hemp seeds. Actually, I don't buy hemp sees at all any more. As someone with celiac I am concerned about the potential for cross contamination of hemp with wheat. Hemp is often grown in areas that grow wheat and the equipment that is used to harvest wheat is used to harvest hemp. Also, hemp is often stored in the same facilities as wheat increasing the chance for cross contamination even more. The chance of significant contamination is small but worth considering if you have celiac.
